      <tr>
       <td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-bottom:#aaa 1px solid;">
        <span class="metadata">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Indonesia.svg">
          <img alt="Portal icon" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/16px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/24px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/32px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>
         </a>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Portal:Indonesia" title="Portal:Indonesia">
         Indonesia portal
        </a>

      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Indonesia" title="Template:History of Indonesia">
            <span title="View this template">
             v
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Indonesia" title="Template talk:History of Indonesia">
            <span title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Indonesia&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <b>
       Srivijaya
      </b>
      (also written
      <b>
       Sri Vijaya
      </b>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">
       Indonesian
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
       Malay
      </a>
      :
      <b>
       Sriwijaya
      </b>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">
       Thai
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="th" xml:lang="th">
       
      </span>
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_General_System_of_Transcription" title="Royal Thai General System of Transcription">
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        rtgs
       </span>
      </a>
      :
      <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Thai transliteration">
       Siwichai
      </span>
      ) was a dominant
      <a href="/wiki/Thalassocracy" title="Thalassocracy">
       thalassocratic
      </a>
      city-state based on the island of
      <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">
       Sumatra
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
       Indonesia
      </a>
      , which influenced much of
      <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">
       Southeast Asia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-end_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-end-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Srivijaya was an important centre for the expansion of
      <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">
       Buddhism
      </a>
      from the 8th to the 12th century. In Sanskrit,
      <i>
       sri
      </i>
      means "fortunate", "prosperous", or "happy" and
      <i>
       vijaya
      </i>
      means "victorious" or "excellence".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_117_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_117-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest evidence of its existence dates from the 7th century: a Chinese monk,
      <a href="/wiki/Yijing_(monk)" title="Yijing (monk)">
       Yijing
      </a>
      , wrote that he visited Srivijaya in 671 for 6 months.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The earliest known inscription in which the name
      <i>
       Srivijaya
      </i>
      appears also dates from the 7th century, i.e., the
      <a href="/wiki/Kedukan_Bukit_inscription" title="Kedukan Bukit inscription">
       Kedukan Bukit inscription
      </a>
      found near
      <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">
       Palembang
      </a>
      , Sumatra, dated 16 June 682.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Between the late 7th to early 11th century Srivijaya rose to become a
      <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">
       hegemon
      </a>
      in Southeast Asia, involved in close interactions  often rivalries  with the neighboring
      <a href="/wiki/Medang_Kingdom" title="Medang Kingdom">
       Medang Kingdom
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">
       Khmer Empire
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">
       Champa
      </a>
      . Srivijaya's main foreign interest was nurturing lucrative trade agreements with
      <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
       China
      </a>
      which continued from the
      <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">
       Tang dynasty
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">
       Song dynasty
      </a>
      . Srivijaya had religious, cultural and trade links with the Buddhist
      <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">
       Pala Empire
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">
       Bengal
      </a>
      , as well as with the Islamic Caliphate in the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
       Middle East
      </a>
      . The kingdom ceased to exist in the 13th century due to various factors, including the expansion of the
      <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">
       Javanese
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Singhasari" title="Singhasari">
       Singhasari
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">
       Majapahit
      </a>
      empires.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-end_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-end-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After Srivijaya fell, it was largely forgotten. It was not until 1918 that French historian
      <a href="/wiki/George_Coed%C3%A8s" title="George Coeds">
       George Coeds
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_fran%C3%A7aise_d%27Extr%C3%AAme-Orient" title="cole franaise d'Extrme-Orient">
       cole franaise d'Extrme-Orient
      </a>
      formally postulated its existence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_117_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_117-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An aerial photograph taken in 1984 near
      <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">
       Palembang
      </a>
      (in what is now
      <a href="/wiki/Sriwijaya_Kingdom_Archaeological_Park" title="Sriwijaya Kingdom Archaeological Park">
       Sriwijaya Kingdom Archaeological Park
      </a>
      ) revealed the remnants of ancient man-made canals, moats, ponds, and artificial islands, suggesting the location of Srivijaya's urban center. Several artifacts such as fragments of inscriptions, Buddhist statues, beads, pottery and Chinese ceramics were found, confirming that the area was once a dense human habitation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinas_Pendidikan_Provinsi_Sumatera_Selatan_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dinas_Pendidikan_Provinsi_Sumatera_Selatan-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1993,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pierre-Yves_Manguin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pierre-Yves Manguin (page does not exist)">
       Pierre-Yves Manguin
      </a>
      had shown that the centre of Srivijaya was along the
      <a href="/wiki/Musi_River_(Indonesia)" title="Musi River (Indonesia)">
       Musi River
      </a>
      between
      <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Seguntang" title="Bukit Seguntang">
       Bukit Seguntang
      </a>
      and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sabokingking&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sabokingking (page does not exist)">
       Sabokingking
      </a>
      (situated in what is now Palembang,
      <a href="/wiki/South_Sumatra" title="South Sumatra">
       South Sumatra
      </a>
      , Indonesia).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_117_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_117-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, in 2013, archaeological research led by the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Indonesia" title="University of Indonesia">
       University of Indonesia
      </a>
      discovered several religious and habitation sites at
      <a href="/wiki/Muaro_Jambi_Temple_Compounds" title="Muaro Jambi Temple Compounds">
       Muaro Jambi
      </a>
      , suggesting that the initial center of Srivijaya was located in
      <a href="/wiki/Muaro_Jambi_Regency" title="Muaro Jambi Regency">
       Muaro Jambi Regency
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jambi" title="Jambi">
       Jambi
      </a>
      on the
      <a href="/wiki/Batang_Hari_River" title="Batang Hari River">
       Batang Hari River
      </a>
      , instead of on the originally-proposed Musi river.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">
       Historiography
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Historiography">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1941" data-file-width="2551" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg/220px-Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg/330px-Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg/440px-Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Talang_Tuo_Inscription.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Talang_Tuwo_inscription" title="Talang Tuwo inscription">
         Talang Tuwo inscription
        </a>
        , discovered in
        <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Seguntang" title="Bukit Seguntang">
         Bukit Seguntang
        </a>
        area, tell the establishment of sacred rksetra park.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      There was no continuous knowledge of Srivijaya even in Indonesian histories; its forgotten past has been recreated by foreign scholars. No modern Indonesians, not even those of the Palembang area around which the kingdom was based, had heard of Srivijaya until the 1920s, when French scholar
      <a href="/wiki/George_Coed%C3%A8s" title="George Coeds">
       George Coeds
      </a>
      published his discoveries and interpretations in Dutch and Indonesian-language newspapers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAYLOR_26_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TAYLOR_26-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Coeds noted that the Chinese references to "Sanfoqi", previously read as "Sribhoja", and the inscriptions in
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
       Old Malay
      </a>
      refer to the same empire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The historical records of Srivijaya were reconstructed from numbers of stone inscriptions, most of them written in Old Malay, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Kedukan_Bukit_inscription" title="Kedukan Bukit inscription">
       Kedukan Bukit
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Talang_Tuwo_inscription" title="Talang Tuwo inscription">
       Talang Tuwo
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Telaga_Batu_inscription" title="Telaga Batu inscription">
       Telaga Batu
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Kota_Kapur_inscription" title="Kota Kapur inscription">
       Kota Kapur inscriptions
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coedes_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Coedes-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :8283
      </sup>
      Srivijaya has become a symbol of early Sumatran importance as a great empire to balance Java's
      <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">
       Majapahit
      </a>
      in the east. In the 20th century, both empires were referred to by nationalist intellectuals to argue for an Indonesian identity within an Indonesian state prior to the colonial state of the
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">
       Dutch East Indies
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAYLOR_26_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-TAYLOR_26-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Srivijaya and by extension Sumatra had been known by different names to different peoples. The Chinese called it
      <i>
       Sanfoqi
      </i>
      , and there was an even older kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Kantoli" title="Kantoli">
       Kantoli
      </a>
      that could be considered the predecessor of Srivijaya.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_114_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_114-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sanskrit and Pali referred to it as Yavadesh and Javadeh, respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_114_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_114-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Arabs called it the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zabag_Kingdom" title="Zabag Kingdom">
       Zabag Kingdom
      </a>
      and the Khmer called it Melayu.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_114_11-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_114-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is another reason why the discovery of Srivijaya was so difficult.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_114_11-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_114-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While some of these names are strongly reminiscent of the name of
      <i>
       Java
      </i>
      , there is a distinct possibility that they may have referred to Sumatra instead.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_growth">
       Formation and growth
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Formation and growth">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="563" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg/220px-Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg/330px-Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg/440px-Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Candi_Gumpung_Muarojambi.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Candi Gumpung, a Buddhist temple at the
        <a href="/wiki/Muaro_Jambi_Temple_Compounds" title="Muaro Jambi Temple Compounds">
         Muaro Jambi Temple Compounds
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Melayu_Kingdom" title="Melayu Kingdom">
         Melayu Kingdom
        </a>
        , later integrated as one of Srivijaya's important urban center.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Little physical evidence of Srivijaya remains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the
      <a href="/wiki/Kedukan_Bukit_inscription" title="Kedukan Bukit inscription">
       Kedukan Bukit inscription
      </a>
      , dated 605 Saka (683 CE), the empire of Srivijaya was founded by
      <a href="/wiki/Dapunta_Hyang_Sri_Jayanasa" title="Dapunta Hyang Sri Jayanasa">
       Dapunta Hyang Sri Jayanasa
      </a>
      . He embarked in a sacred
      <i>
       siddhayatra
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      journey, and led 20,000 troops and 312 people in boats with 1312 foot soldiers from Minanga Tamwan to
      <a href="/wiki/Jambi" title="Jambi">
       Jambi
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">
       Palembang
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Although according to this inscription, Srivijaya was first established in the vicinity of today's Palembang, it mentions that Dapunta Hyang Sri Jayanasa came from Minanga Tamwan. The exact location of Minanga Tamwan is still a subject of discussion. The Palembang theory as the place where Srivijaya was first established, was presented by Coedes and supported by Pierre-Yves Manguin. Soekmono on the other hand, argues that Palembang is not the capital of Srivijaya and suggests that the
      <a href="/wiki/Kampar_River" title="Kampar River">
       Kampar River
      </a>
      system in
      <a href="/wiki/Riau" title="Riau">
       Riau
      </a>
      where the
      <a href="/wiki/Muara_Takus" title="Muara Takus">
       Muara Takus
      </a>
      temple is located as Minanga Tamwan.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soekmono_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Soekmono-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another theory suggests that Dapunta Hyang came from the east coast of the
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">
       Malay Peninsula
      </a>
      , and suggests the
      <a href="/wiki/Chaiya_District" title="Chaiya District">
       Chaiya District
      </a>
      as the center of Srivijaya.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Around the year 500, Srivijayan roots began to develop around present-day
      <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">
       Palembang
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">
       Sumatra
      </a>
      , modern
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
       Indonesia
      </a>
      . The empire was organised in three main zones: the estuarine capital region centred on Palembang, the
      <a href="/wiki/Musi_River_(Indonesia)" title="Musi River (Indonesia)">
       Musi River
      </a>
      basin which served as hinterland and rival estuarine areas capable of forming rival power centres. The areas upstream of the Musi River were rich in various commodities valuable to Chinese traders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The capital was administered directly by the ruler while the hinterland remained under its own local
      <a href="/wiki/Datu" title="Datu">
       datus
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">
       tribal chiefs
      </a>
      , who were organized into a network of alliances with the Srivijaya
      <a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">
       maharaja
      </a>
      or king. Force was the dominant element in the empire's relations with rival river systems such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Batang_Hari_River" title="Batang Hari River">
       Batang Hari River
      </a>
      , centred in Jambi.
     </p>
     <p>
      From Sanskrit inscriptions, it's notable that Dapunta Hyang Sri Jayanasa launched a maritime conquest in 684 with 20,000 men in the
      <i>
       siddhayatra
      </i>
      journey to acquire wealth, power, and 'magic power'.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Under the leadership of Dapunta Hyang Sri Jayanasa, the
      <a href="/wiki/Melayu_Kingdom" title="Melayu Kingdom">
       Melayu Kingdom
      </a>
      became the first kingdom to be integrated into Srivijaya. This possibly occurred in the 680s. Melayu, also known as Jambi, was rich in gold and was held in high esteem. Srivijaya recognized that the submission of Melayu would increase its own prestige.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to the
      <a href="/wiki/Kota_Kapur_inscription" title="Kota Kapur inscription">
       Kota Kapur inscription
      </a>
      discovered on
      <a href="/wiki/Bangka_Island" title="Bangka Island">
       Bangka Island
      </a>
      , the empire conquered most of southern Sumatra and the neighboring island of Bangka, as far as
      <a href="/wiki/Lampung" title="Lampung">
       Lampung
      </a>
      . Also according to the inscription, Dapunta Hyang Sri Jayanasa launched a military campaign against Java in the late 7th century, a period which coincides with the decline of
      <a href="/wiki/Tarumanagara" title="Tarumanagara">
       Tarumanagara
      </a>
      in West Java and the
      <a href="/wiki/Kalingga_Kingdom" title="Kalingga Kingdom">
       Kalingga Kingdom
      </a>
      in Central Java. The empire thus grew to control the trade on the
      <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">
       Strait of Malacca
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait">
       Sunda Strait
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">
       South China Sea
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Java_Sea" title="Java Sea">
       Java Sea
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Karimata_Strait" title="Karimata Strait">
       Karimata Strait
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Chinese records dating to the late 7th century mention two Sumatran kingdoms, as well as three other kingdoms on Java as part of Srivijaya. By the end of the 8th century, many western Javanese kingdoms, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Tarumanagara" title="Tarumanagara">
       Tarumanagara
      </a>
      and Kalingga, were within the Srivijayan sphere of influence. It has also been recorded that a Buddhist family related to Srivijaya dominated central Java at that time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The family was probably the
      <a href="/wiki/Sailendra" title="Sailendra">
       Sailendras
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_132_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_132-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ruling
      <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">
       lineage
      </a>
      of Srivijaya intermarried with the Sailendras of Central Java and lived along the Javanese
      <a href="/wiki/Sanjaya_dynasty" title="Sanjaya dynasty">
       Sanjaya dynasty
      </a>
      when the Srivijayan capital was located in Java.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the same century, Langkasuka on the Malay Peninsula became part of Srivijaya.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soon after this, Pan Pan and Trambralinga, which were located north of Langkasuka, came under Srivijayan influence. These kingdoms on the peninsula were major trading nations that transported goods across the peninsula's isthmus.
     </p>
     <p>
      With the expansion into Java and the Malay Peninsula, Srivijaya controlled two major trade
      <a href="/wiki/Choke_point" title="Choke point">
       choke points
      </a>
      in Southeast Asia. Some Srivijayan temple ruins are observable in
      <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
       Thailand
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">
       Cambodia
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The area of Chaiya District in
      <a href="/wiki/Surat_Thani_Province" title="Surat Thani Province">
       Surat Thani Province
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
       Thailand
      </a>
      , was already inhabited in prehistoric times by
      <a href="/wiki/Semang" title="Semang">
       Semang
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_Malays" title="Ethnic Malays">
       Ethnic Malays
      </a>
      . Founded in the 3rd century, Srivijaya dominated the
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">
       Malay Peninsula
      </a>
      and much of the island of
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Java
      </a>
      from there until the 13th century. The city of Chaiya's name may be derived from the Malay name "Cahaya". However, some scholars believe that
      <i>
       Chaiya
      </i>
      probably comes from Sri Vi
      <b>
       jaya.
      </b>
      It was a regional capital in the Srivijaya empire of the 5th to 13th century. Some Thai historians argue it was the capital of Srivijaya itself, but this is generally discounted.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg/220px-001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg/330px-001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg/440px-001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:001_View_of_Borobudur.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The construction of the
        <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">
         Borobudur
        </a>
        completed under the reign of Samaratunga of Sailendra dynasty.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      At some point in the 7th century, Cham ports in eastern Indochina started to attract traders. This diverted the flow of trade from Srivijaya. In an effort to divert the flow, the Srivijayan king or
      <a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">
       maharaja
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dharmasetu" title="Dharmasetu">
       Dharmasetu
      </a>
      , launched various raids against the coastal cities of Indochina. The city of
      <a href="/wiki/Indrapura_(Champa)" title="Indrapura (Champa)">
       Indrapura
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/Mekong" title="Mekong">
       Mekong
      </a>
      was temporarily controlled from Palembang in the early 8th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_132_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_132-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Srivijayans continued to dominate areas around present-day Cambodia until the
      <a href="/wiki/Khmer_people" title="Khmer people">
       Khmer
      </a>
      King
      <a href="/wiki/Jayavarman_II" title="Jayavarman II">
       Jayavarman II
      </a>
      , the founder of the
      <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">
       Khmer Empire
      </a>
      dynasty, severed the Srivijayan link later in the same century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After Dharmasetu,
      <a href="/wiki/Samaratungga" title="Samaratungga">
       Samaratungga
      </a>
      became the next Maharaja of Srivijaya. He reigned as ruler from 792 to 835. Unlike the expansionist Dharmasetu, Samaratungga did not indulge in military expansion but preferred to strengthen the Srivijayan hold of
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Java
      </a>
      . He personally oversaw the construction of
      <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">
       Borobudur
      </a>
      ; the temple was completed in 825, during his reign.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Coedes" title="George Coedes">
       George Coedes
      </a>
      , "in the second half of the ninth century Java and Sumatra were united under the rule of a Sailendra reigning in Java...its center at Palembang."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coedes_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Coedes-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :92
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thalassocratic_empire">
       Thalassocratic empire
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Thalassocratic empire">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Srivijayan_Expansion.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="362" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Srivijayan_Expansion.gif/220px-Srivijayan_Expansion.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Srivijayan_Expansion.gif/330px-Srivijayan_Expansion.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Srivijayan_Expansion.gif 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Srivijayan_Expansion.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Expansion of Srivijayan empire, started in Palembang in 7th century, expanding throughout Sumatra, Malay Peninsula, Java, Cambodia, and receded as Malayu Dharmasraya in the 13th century.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Srivijayan empire was a coastal trading centre and was a
      <a href="/wiki/Thalassocracy" title="Thalassocracy">
       thalassocracy
      </a>
      . As such, it did not extend its influence far beyond the coastal areas of the islands of
      <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">
       Southeast Asia
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The Srivijayan was benefited from the lucrative maritime trade between China and India, and also trading Indonesian archipelago product such as
      <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">
       Maluku
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">
       spices
      </a>
      . Served as Southeast Asia's main
      <a href="/wiki/Entrep%C3%B4t" title="Entrept">
       entrept
      </a>
      and gain trade patronage appointed by Chinese court, Srivijaya was constantly managing their trade network and always wary of potential rival ports of neighboring kingdoms. The necessity to maintain their trade monopoly has led them to launch naval military expeditions against rival ports in Southeast Asia, and absorb them within Srivijayan mandala. The port of Malayu in Jambi, Kota Kapur in Bangka island, Tarumanagara and port of Sunda in West Java, Kalingga in Central Java, and port of Kedah and Chaiya in Malay peninsula are among regional ports that being absorbed within Srivijayan sphere of influence. Series of Javan-Srivijaya raids on ports of
      <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">
       Champa
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">
       Cambodia
      </a>
      was also their effort to maintain their monopoly in the region by sacking rival ports.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_ship.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="1183" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Borobudur_ship.JPG/220px-Borobudur_ship.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Borobudur_ship.JPG/330px-Borobudur_ship.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Borobudur_ship.JPG/440px-Borobudur_ship.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_ship.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The image of a
        <a href="/wiki/Borobudur_ship" title="Borobudur ship">
         Borobudur ship
        </a>
        on bas relief
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The maritime prowess was recorded in a
      <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">
       Borobudur
      </a>
      bas relief of
      <a href="/wiki/Borobudur_ship" title="Borobudur ship">
       Borobudur ship
      </a>
      , the 8th century wooden double
      <a href="/wiki/Outrigger" title="Outrigger">
       outrigger
      </a>
      vehicles of
      <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">
       Maritime Southeast Asia
      </a>
      . The function of outrigger is to stabilize the ship, the single or double
      <a href="/wiki/Outrigger_canoe" title="Outrigger canoe">
       outrigger canoe
      </a>
      is the typical feature of the seafaring
      <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">
       Austronesians
      </a>
      vessels and the most likely the type of vessel used for their voyages and exploration across
      <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">
       Southeast Asia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">
       Oceania
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
       Indian Ocean
      </a>
      . The ships depicted at Borobudur most likely were the type of vessels used for inter-insular trades and naval campaign by
      <a href="/wiki/Sailendra" title="Sailendra">
       Sailendran
      </a>
      and Srivijayan thalassocracy empire that ruled the region from the 7th to 13th centuries.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Srivijayan empire mainly exercised its influence around coastal areas of Southeast Asia, with the exception of contributing to the population of
      <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">
       Madagascar
      </a>
      3,300 miles (8,000 kilometres) to the west.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_Madagascar_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-History_of_Madagascar-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The migration to Madagascar was estimated took place 1200 years ago around 830 CE. According to an extensive new mitochondrial DNA study, native Malagasy people today can likely trace their heritage back to the 30 founding mothers sailed from Indonesia 1200 years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madagascar_Founded_By_Women_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Madagascar_Founded_By_Women-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Malagasy contains loan words from Sanskrit, all with local linguistic modifications via
      <a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">
       Javanese
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
       Malay
      </a>
      , hint that Madagascar may have been colonized by settlers from the Srivijaya empire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_small_cohort_of_Island_Southeast_Asian_women_founded_Madagascar_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-A_small_cohort_of_Island_Southeast_Asian_women_founded_Madagascar-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At that time the Srivijayan maritime empire was expanding their maritime trade network.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wanita_Indonesia_Nenek_Moyang_Penduduk_Madagaskar_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wanita_Indonesia_Nenek_Moyang_Penduduk_Madagaskar-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_regional_powers">
       Relationship with regional powers
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Relationship with regional powers">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1701" data-file-width="937" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG/170px-Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG/255px-Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG/340px-Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avalokiteshvara_Bingin_Jungut_Srivijaya.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Avalokiteshvara from Bingin Jungut,
        <a href="/wiki/Musi_Rawas_Regency" title="Musi Rawas Regency">
         Musi Rawas Regency
        </a>
        , South Sumatra. Srivijayan art (c. 8th-9th century CE) bears a close resemblance to Central Java
        <a href="/wiki/Sailendra" title="Sailendra">
         Sailendran
        </a>
        art.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although historical records and archaeological evidence are scarce, it appears that by the 7th century, Srivijaya had established suzerainty over large areas of Sumatra, western Java and much of the Malay Peninsula. Dominating the
      <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">
       Malacca
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait">
       Sunda Straits
      </a>
      , Srivijaya controlled both the
      <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">
       spice route
      </a>
      traffic and local trade, charging a toll on passing ships. Serving as an entrept for Chinese, Malay, and Indian markets, the port of Palembang, accessible from the coast by way of a river, accumulated great wealth. Envoys travelled to and from China frequently.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Melayu Kingdom was the first rival power centre absorbed into the empire, and thus began the domination of the region through trade and conquest in the 7th and 9th centuries. Malayu kingdom's gold mines up in
      <a href="/wiki/Batang_Hari_River" title="Batang Hari River">
       Batang Hari River
      </a>
      hinterland were a crucial economic resource and may be the origin of the word
      <a href="/wiki/Suvarnabhumi" title="Suvarnabhumi">
       Suvarnadvipa
      </a>
      , the Sanskrit name for Sumatra. Srivijaya helped spread the Malay culture throughout Sumatra, the Malay Peninsula, and western
      <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">
       Borneo
      </a>
      . Srivijaya's influence waned in the 11th century. It was in frequent conflict with, and ultimately subjugated by,
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Javanese
      </a>
      kingdoms, first
      <a href="/wiki/Singhasari" title="Singhasari">
       Singhasari
      </a>
      and then
      <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">
       Majapahit
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was not the first time the Srivijayans conflicted with the Javanese. According to historian
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paul_Michel_Munoz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paul Michel Munoz (page does not exist)">
       Paul Michel Munoz
      </a>
      , the Javanese
      <a href="/wiki/Sanjaya_dynasty" title="Sanjaya dynasty">
       Sanjaya dynasty
      </a>
      was a strong rival of the Srivijayans in the 8th century when the Srivijayan capital was located in
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Java
      </a>
      . The seat of the empire moved to
      <a href="/wiki/Muaro_Jambi_Regency" title="Muaro Jambi Regency">
       Muaro Jambi
      </a>
      in the last centuries of Srivijaya's existence.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2551" data-file-width="1787" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG/170px-Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG/255px-Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG/340px-Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avalokiteshvara_Srivijaya_Art_Chaiya.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The bronze torso statue of the bodhisattva
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Padmapani" title="Padmapani">
         Padmapani
        </a>
        , 8th century CE Srivijayan art,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaiya" title="Chaiya">
         Chaiya
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Surat_Thani" title="Surat Thani">
         Surat Thani
        </a>
        , Southern Thailand. The statue demonstrate the Central Java (
        <a href="/wiki/Sailendra" title="Sailendra">
         Sailendra
        </a>
        ) art influence.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
