          <img alt="Portal icon" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2800" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flag_of_Malaysia.svg/16px-Flag_of_Malaysia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flag_of_Malaysia.svg/24px-Flag_of_Malaysia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flag_of_Malaysia.svg/32px-Flag_of_Malaysia.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>
         </a>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Portal:Malaysia" title="Portal:Malaysia">
         Malaysia portal
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Malaysia" title="Template:History of Malaysia">
            <span title="View this template">
             v
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Malaysia" title="Template talk:History of Malaysia">
            <span title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Malaysia&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">
       Khmer Empire
      </a>
      might also have been a
      <a href="/wiki/Tributary_state" title="Tributary state">
       tributary state
      </a>
      in its early stages. The Khmer king
      <a href="/wiki/Jayavarman_II" title="Jayavarman II">
       Jayavarman II
      </a>
      , was mentioned has spent years in the court of Sailendra in Java, and came home to rule Cambodia around 790 CE. Influenced by Javanese culture of Sailendran-Srivijayan mandala, probably with the eagerness to copy the Javanese model in his court, he proclaimed Cambodian independence from Java and ruled as
      <a href="/wiki/Devaraja" title="Devaraja">
       devaraja
      </a>
      , established Khmer empire and started the
      <a href="/wiki/Angkor" title="Angkor">
       Angkor
      </a>
      era.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some historians claim that Chaiya in
      <a href="/wiki/Surat_Thani_Province" title="Surat Thani Province">
       Surat Thani Province
      </a>
      in southern Thailand was at least temporarily the capital of Srivijaya, but this claim is widely disputed. However, Chaiya was probably a regional centre of the kingdom. The temple of Borom That in Chaiya contains a reconstructed
      <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">
       pagoda
      </a>
      in Srivijaya style.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muljana_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Muljana-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wat_Phra_Boromathat_Chaiya&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wat Phra Boromathat Chaiya (page does not exist)">
       Wat Phra Boromathat Chaiya
      </a>
      is highlighted by the pagoda in Srivijaya style, dating back from the 7th century but elaborately restored. Buddha relics are enshrined in the chedi, in the surrounding chapels are several Buddha statues in Srivijaya style as it was labeled by
      <a href="/wiki/Damrong_Rajanubhab" title="Damrong Rajanubhab">
       Damrong Rajanubhab
      </a>
      in his
      <i>
       Collected Inscriptions of Siam
      </i>
      , is now attributed to Wat Hua Wiang in Chaiya. Dated to the year 697 of the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mahasakkarat_era&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mahasakkarat era (page does not exist)">
       Mahasakkarat era
      </a>
      (i.e. 775 CE), the inscription on a
      <a href="/wiki/Bai_sema" title="Bai sema">
       bai sema
      </a>
      tells about the King of Srivijaya having erected three stupas at that site that possibly the one at Wat Phra Borom That. But also be assumed as three stupas at Wat Hua Wiang (Hua Wiang temple), Wat Lhong (Lhong temple) and Wat Kaew (Kaew temple) found in the area of Chaiya ancient city, stand in the direction from north to south on the old sand dune. After the fall of the Srivijaya in Chaiya, the area was divided into the cities (
      <a href="/wiki/Mueang" title="Mueang">
       mueang
      </a>
      ) Chaiya, Thatong (now Kanchanadit) and Khirirat Nikhom.
     </p>
     <p>
      Srivijaya also maintained close relations with the
      <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">
       Pala Empire
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">
       Bengal
      </a>
      , and an 860
      <a href="/wiki/Nalanda_inscription" title="Nalanda inscription">
       Nalanda inscription
      </a>
      records that maharaja Balaputra dedicated a monastery at the
      <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">
       Nalanda university
      </a>
      in Pala territory.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coedes_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Coedes-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :109
      </sup>
      The relation between Srivijaya and the
      <a href="/wiki/Chola_dynasty" title="Chola dynasty">
       Chola dynasty
      </a>
      of southern India was initially friendly during the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Raja_Raja_Chola_I" title="Raja Raja Chola I">
       Raja Raja Chola I
      </a>
      . In 1006 CE a Srivijayan Maharaja from Sailendra dynasty, king Maravijayattungavarman, constructed the
      <a href="/wiki/Chudamani_Vihara" title="Chudamani Vihara">
       Chudamani Vihara
      </a>
      in the port town of
      <a href="/wiki/Nagapattinam" title="Nagapattinam">
       Nagapattinam
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sastri219220_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sastri219220-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, during the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Rajendra_Chola_I" title="Rajendra Chola I">
       Rajendra Chola I
      </a>
      the relations deteriorate as the Chola Dynasty started to attack Srivijayan cities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The reason of this sudden change of relations with Chola is unknown. However some historian suggests, it seems that the Khmer king Suryavarman I of the
      <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">
       Khmer Empire
      </a>
      requested aid from Emperor
      <a href="/wiki/Rajendra_Chola_I" title="Rajendra Chola I">
       Rajendra Chola I
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Chola_dynasty" title="Chola dynasty">
       Chola dynasty
      </a>
      against Tambralinga.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenneth_R._Hall_1975_pp._318-336_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_R._Hall_1975_pp._318-336-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After learning of Suryavarman's alliance with Rajendra Chola, the Tambralinga kingdom requested aid from the Srivijaya king Sangrama Vijayatungavarman.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenneth_R._Hall_1975_pp._318-336_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_R._Hall_1975_pp._318-336-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C._Majumdar_1961_pp._338-342_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-C._Majumdar_1961_pp._338-342-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This eventually led to the Chola Empire coming into conflict with the Srivijiya Empire. The war ended with a victory for the Chola and losses for the Srivijaya.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenneth_R._Hall_1975_pp._318-336_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kenneth_R._Hall_1975_pp._318-336-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C._Majumdar_1961_pp._338-342_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-C._Majumdar_1961_pp._338-342-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Kulothunga_Chola_I" title="Kulothunga Chola I">
       Kulothunga Chola I
      </a>
      the king of Srivijaya sent an embassy to the Chola Dynasty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buddhism.2C_Diplomacy_p.226_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Buddhism.2C_Diplomacy_p.226-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Arab writers of the 9th and 10th century hardly mention Europe for anything other than its backwardness but they consider the king of Al-Hind (India and to some extent might include Southeast Asia) as one of the 4 great kings in the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancien_Regime_p.41_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ancien_Regime_p.41-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-Hind_p.226_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Al-Hind_p.226-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ruler of the
      <a href="/wiki/Rashtrakuta_dynasty" title="Rashtrakuta dynasty">
       Rashtrakuta dynasty
      </a>
      is described as the greatest king of Al-Hind (India) but moreover to further extent, the kings of Al-Hind might also including the kings of Java, Pagan Burma and the Khmer kings of Cambodia are invariably depicted by the Arabs as extremely powerful and as being equipped with vast armies of men, horses and often tens of thousands of elephants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancien_Regime_p.41_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ancien_Regime_p.41-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-Hind_p.226_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Al-Hind_p.226-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are also known to be in the possession of vast treasures of gold and silver.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ancien_Regime_p.41_39-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ancien_Regime_p.41-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-Hind_p.226_40-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Al-Hind_p.226-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_age">
       Golden age
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Golden age">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avalokite%C3%A7vara,_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2441" data-file-width="1331" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Avalokite%C3%A7vara%2C_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg/170px-Avalokite%C3%A7vara%2C_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Avalokite%C3%A7vara%2C_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg/255px-Avalokite%C3%A7vara%2C_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Avalokite%C3%A7vara%2C_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg/340px-Avalokite%C3%A7vara%2C_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avalokite%C3%A7vara,_Malayu_Srivijaya_style.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The graceful golden statue of
        <a href="/wiki/Avalokite%C5%9Bvara" title="Avalokitevara">
         Avalokitevara
        </a>
        in Malayu-Srivijayan style, discovered at Rataukapastuo, Muarabulian,
        <a href="/wiki/Jambi" title="Jambi">
         Jambi
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
         Indonesia
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      After trade disruption at
      <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">
       Canton
      </a>
      between 820 and 850, the ruler of Jambi (
      <a href="/wiki/Melayu_Kingdom" title="Melayu Kingdom">
       Melayu Kingdom
      </a>
      ) was able to assert enough independence to send missions to
      <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
       China
      </a>
      in 853 and 871.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2008)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Melayu kingdom's independence coincided with the troubled time when the
      <a href="/wiki/Sailendra" title="Sailendra">
       Sailendran
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balaputradewa" title="Balaputradewa">
       Balaputradewa
      </a>
      , expelled from Java, seized the throne of Srivijaya. The new maharaja was able to dispatch a tributary mission to China by 902. Only two years later, the expiring
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">
       Tang Dynasty
      </a>
      conferred a title on a Srivijayan envoy.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the first half of the 10th century, between the fall of Tang and the rise of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">
       Song
      </a>
      , there was brisk trade between the overseas world and the
      <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">
       Fujian
      </a>
      kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Min_(Ten_Kingdoms)" title="Min (Ten Kingdoms)">
       Min
      </a>
      and the rich
      <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">
       Guangdong
      </a>
      kingdom of Nan Han. Srivijaya undoubtedly benefited from this, in anticipation of the prosperity it was to enjoy under the early Song. Circa 903, the
      <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
       Muslim
      </a>
      writer
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Rustah" title="Ibn Rustah">
       Ibn Rustah
      </a>
      was so impressed with the wealth of Srivijaya's ruler that he declared one would not hear of a king who was richer, stronger or with more revenue. The main urban centres were at Palembang (especially the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Srivijaya_archaeological_park" title="Srivijaya archaeological park">
       Karanganyar site
      </a>
      near
      <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Seguntang" title="Bukit Seguntang">
       Bukit Seguntang
      </a>
      area),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muara_Jambi" title="Muara Jambi">
       Muara Jambi
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Kedah" title="Kedah">
       Kedah
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The migration to
      <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">
       Madagascar
      </a>
      accelerated in the 9th century, when the powerful Sumatran empire of Srivijaya controlled much of the maritime trade in the
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
       Indian Ocean
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_Madagascar_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-History_of_Madagascar-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In late 10th century the rivalry between Sumatran Srivijaya and Javanese
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medang" title="Medang">
       Medang
      </a>
      kingdom has become more intense and hostile. The animosity was probably caused by Srivijaya effort to reclaim Sailendra lands in Java, as Balaputra and his offsprings the series Srivijaya Maharajas was belongs to Sailendra dynasty, or probably led by Medang aspiration to challenge Srivijaya domination in the region. In the year 990, king
      <a href="/wiki/Dharmawangsa" title="Dharmawangsa">
       Dharmawangsa
      </a>
      launched a naval invasion against Srivijaya, and unsuccessfully attempted to capture Palembang. Dharmawangsa's invasion led the Maharaja of Srivijaya, Chulamaniwarmadewa to seek protection from China. In 1006, Srivijaya's mandala alliance proved its resilience by successfully repelling the Javanese invasion. In retaliation, Srivijaya assisted Haji (king) Wurawari of Lwaram to revolt, attacking and destroying the Medang palace. With the death of Dharmawangsa and the fall of the Medang capital, Srivijaya contributed to the collapse of Medang kingdom, leaving Eastern Java in further unrest, violence, and desolation for several years to come.
     </p>
     <p>
      The influence of the empire reached
      <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">
       Manila
      </a>
      by the 10th century. A
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tondo" title="Kingdom of Tondo">
       kingdom
      </a>
      under its sphere of influence had already been established there.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mts_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mts-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bibingka_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bibingka-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By the 12th century, the kingdom included parts of
      <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">
       Sumatra
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">
       Malay Peninsula
      </a>
      , Western
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Java
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">
       Borneo
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
       Philippines
      </a>
      , most notably the
      <a href="/wiki/Sulu_Archipelago" title="Sulu Archipelago">
       Sulu Archipelago
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Visayas" title="Visayas">
       Visayas
      </a>
      islands (and indeed the latter island group, as well as its
      <a href="/wiki/Visayans" title="Visayans">
       population
      </a>
      , is named after the empire).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Srivijaya remained a formidable sea power until the 13th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-end_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-end-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Political_administration">
       Political administration
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Political administration">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2268" data-file-width="2090" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG/220px-Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG/330px-Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG/440px-Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Telaga_Batu_inscription.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Telaga_Batu_inscription" title="Telaga Batu inscription">
         Telaga Batu inscription
        </a>
        adorned with seven
        <a href="/wiki/N%C4%81ga" title="Nga">
         nga
        </a>
        heads on top, and a waterspout on the lower part to channel the water probably poured during ceremonial allegiance ritual.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The 7th century
      <a href="/wiki/Telaga_Batu_inscription" title="Telaga Batu inscription">
       Telaga Batu inscription
      </a>
      discovered in Sabokingking, Palembang, testifies to the complexity and stratified titles of Srivijayan state officials. These titles are mentioned:
      <i>
       rjaputra
      </i>
      (princes, lit: sons of king),
      <i>
       kumrmtya
      </i>
      (ministers),
      <i>
       bhpati
      </i>
      (regional rulers),
      <i>
       senpati
      </i>
      (generals),
      <i>
       nyaka
      </i>
      (local community leaders),
      <i>
       pratyaya
      </i>
      (nobles),
      <i>
       hji pratyaya
      </i>
      (lesser kings),
      <i>
       dandanayaka
      </i>
      (judges),
      <i>
       tuh an vatak
      </i>
      (workers inspectors),
      <i>
       vuruh
      </i>
      (workers),
      <i>
       addhyksi njavarna
      </i>
      (lower supervisors),
      <i>
       vskarana
      </i>
      (blacksmiths/weapon makers),
      <i>
       ctabhata
      </i>
      (soldiers),
      <i>
       adhikarana
      </i>
      (officials),
      <i>
       kyastha
      </i>
      (store workers),
      <i>
       sthpaka
      </i>
      (artisans),
      <i>
       puhvam
      </i>
      (ship captains),
      <i>
       vaniyga
      </i>
      (traders),
      <i>
       mars hji
      </i>
      (king's servants),
      <i>
       hulun hji
      </i>
      (king's slaves).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the formation, the empire was organised in three main zones  the estuarine capital region centred on Palembang, the Musi River basin which served as hinterland and source of valuable goods, and rival estuarine areas capable of forming rival power centres. These rival estuarines through raids and conquests were held under Srivijayan power, such as Batanghari estuarine (
      <a href="/wiki/Melayu_Kingdom" title="Melayu Kingdom">
       Malayu
      </a>
      in Jambi). Several strategic ports follows, such as Bangka island (Kota Kapur), ports and kingdoms in Java (highly possible
      <a href="/wiki/Tarumanagara" title="Tarumanagara">
       Tarumanagara
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalingga" title="Kalingga">
       Kalingga
      </a>
      ), Kedah and Chaiya in Malay peninsula, and Lamuri and Panai in northern Sumatra. There are also reports mentioned the Java-Srivijayan raids on Southern Cambodia (Mekong estuarine) and ports of
      <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">
       Champa
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      After its expansion to neighboring states, the Srivijayan empire was formed as the collection of several
      <a href="/wiki/Kedatuan" title="Kedatuan">
       Kadatuans
      </a>
      (local principalities), all swore allegiance to the central ruling powerful Kadatuan ruled by Srivijayan Maharaja. The political relations and system related to its realms is described as a
      <a href="/wiki/Mandala_(Southeast_Asian_political_model)" title="Mandala (Southeast Asian political model)">
       mandala model
      </a>
      , typical of classical Southeast Asian Hindu-Buddhist kingdoms. It could be described as federation of kingdoms or vassalized polity under a center of domination; central Kadatuan Srivijaya. The polity was defined by its centre rather than its boundaries, and it could be composed of numerous other tributary polities without undergoing further administrative integration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The relations between the central kadatuan and its member (subscribers) kadatuans are dynamic; however, the status may shift over generations. Other than coercive methods through raids and conquests, bound by
      <i>
       persumpahan
      </i>
      (oath of allegiance), the royalties of each kadatuan often formed alliances through dynastic marriages. For example a previously suzerained kadatuan over time might rise in prestige and power, so that eventually its ruler could lay claim to be the maharaja of the central kadatuan. The relationship between Srivijayan in Sumatra (descendants of Dapunta Hyang Sri Jayanasa) and Sailendras in Java describes this political dynamic.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_Culture">
       Art and Culture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Art and Culture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1701" data-file-width="1002" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG/170px-Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG/255px-Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG/340px-Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maitreya_Komering_Srivijaya_Side.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A bronze
        <a href="/wiki/Maitreya" title="Maitreya">
         Maitreya
        </a>
        statue from Komering, South Sumatra, 9th century Srivijayan art.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Buddhist art and architecture of Sri Vijaya was influenced by the Indian art of the
      <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">
       Gupta Empire
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">
       Pala Empire
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to various historical sources, a complex and cosmopolitan society with a refined culture, deeply influenced by Vajrayana Buddhism, flourished in the Srivijayan capital. The 7th century
      <a href="/wiki/Talang_Tuwo_inscription" title="Talang Tuwo inscription">
       Talang Tuwo inscription
      </a>
      described Buddhist rituals and blessings at the auspicious event of establishing public park. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kota_Kapur_Inscription" title="Kota Kapur Inscription">
       Kota Kapur Inscription
      </a>
      mentions Srivijaya military dominance against Java. These inscriptions were in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Malay" title="Old Malay">
       Old Malay
      </a>
      language, the language used by Srivijayan and also the ancestor of
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
       Malay
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">
       Indonesian language
      </a>
      . Since the 7th century, the Old Malay language has been used in
      <a href="/wiki/Nusantara" title="Nusantara">
       Nusantara
      </a>
      (Malay-Indonesian archipelago), marked by these Srivijaya inscriptions and other inscriptions using old Malay language in coastal areas of the archipelago, such as those discovered in Java. The trade contact carried by some ethnics at the time was the main vehicle to spread Malay language, since it was the communication device amongst the traders. By then, Malay language become
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
      </i>
      and was spoken widely by most people in the archipelago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However, despite its economic, cultural and military prowess, Srivijaya left few archaeological remains in their heartlands in Sumatra, in contrast with Srivijayan episode in Central Java during the leadership of
      <a href="/wiki/Sailendra" title="Sailendra">
       Sailendras
      </a>
      that produced numerous monuments; such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candi_Kalasan" title="Candi Kalasan">
       Kalasan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sewu" title="Sewu">
       Sewu
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">
       Borobudur
      </a>
      mandala. The Buddhist temples dated from Srivijayan era in Sumatra are
      <a href="/wiki/Muaro_Jambi_Temple_Compounds" title="Muaro Jambi Temple Compounds">
       Muaro Jambi
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candi_Muara_Takus" title="Candi Muara Takus">
       Muara Takus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bahal_temple" title="Bahal temple">
       Biaro Bahal
      </a>
      , however unlike the temples of Central Java that constructed from andesite stones, the Sumatran temples were constructed from red bricks.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some Buddhist sculptures, such as Buddha
      <a href="/wiki/Vairocana" title="Vairocana">
       Vairocana
      </a>
      , Boddhisattva
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avalokiteshvara" title="Avalokiteshvara">
       Avalokiteshvara
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Maitreya" title="Maitreya">
       Maitreya
      </a>
      , were discovered in numerous sites in Sumatra and Malay Peninsula. These archaeological findings such as stone statue of Buddha discovered in
      <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Seguntang" title="Bukit Seguntang">
       Bukit Seguntang
      </a>
      , Palembang,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Avalokiteshvara from Bingin Jungut in Musi Rawas, bronze Maitreya statue of Komering, all discovered in South Sumatra. In Jambi, golden statue of Avalokiteshvara were discovered in Rataukapastuo, Muarabulian.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Malay Peninsula the bronze statue of Avalokiteshvara of
      <a href="/wiki/Bidor" title="Bidor">
       Bidor
      </a>
      discovered in
      <a href="/wiki/Perak" title="Perak">
       Perak
      </a>
      Malaysia,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Avalokiteshvara of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaiya" title="Chaiya">
       Chaiya
      </a>
      in Southern Thailand.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      All of these statues demonstrated the same elegance and common style identified as "Srivijayan art" that reflects close resemblance probably inspired by both Indian Amaravati style and Javanese Sailendra art (c. 8th to 9th century).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
       Religion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Religion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:45%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
      <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
       <div>
        <p>
         ".... many of kings and rulers in the islands of southern seas adore and believed in lord Buddha, in their hearts has flourished (the seeds of) good deeds. Within the walls of Srivijaya capital city lived 1000 Buddhist monks, they have studied diligently and performed (the noble teachings) very well.... If a Chinese monk wished to travel to India and seeks the (Buddha's) teachings, it will be better for them to stay here first for a year or two, to deepening their knowledge before continued their study to India.".
        </p>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="text-align: right;">
        Description of Srivijaya according to I-tsing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takakusu_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Takakusu-54">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         54
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Srivijaya and its kings were instrumental in the spread of
      <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">
       Buddhism
      </a>
      as they established it in places they conquered like
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Java
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">
       Malaya
      </a>
      , and other lands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bentley_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bentley-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      People making pilgrimages were encouraged to spend time with the monks in the capital city of
      <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">
       Palembang
      </a>
      on their journey to
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       India
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bentley_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bentley-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3289" data-file-width="1701" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg/170px-Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg/255px-Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg/340px-Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_Seguntang_Palembang.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A 2.77 metres tall statue of Buddha in Amaravati style, from
        <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Seguntang" title="Bukit Seguntang">
         Bukit Seguntang
        </a>
        , Palembang, c. 7th8th century.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A stronghold of
      <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">
       Vajrayana
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">
       Buddhism
      </a>
      , Srivijaya attracted pilgrims and scholars from other parts of Asia. These included the Chinese monk
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I_Ching_(monk)" title="I Ching (monk)">
       I Ching
      </a>
      , who made several lengthy visits to Sumatra on his way to study at
      <a href="/wiki/Nalanda_University" title="Nalanda University">
       Nalanda University
      </a>
      in India in 671 and 695, and the 11th century
      <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">
       Bengali
      </a>
      Buddhist scholar
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atisha" title="Atisha">
       Atisha
      </a>
      , who played a major role in the development of Vajrayana Buddhism in
      <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">
       Tibet
      </a>
      . I Ching also known as Yijing and other monks of his time practiced a pure version of Buddhism although the religion allowed for culture changes to be made.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He is also given credit for translating Buddhist text which has the most instructions on the discipline of the religion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      I Ching reports that the kingdom was home to more than a thousand Buddhist scholars; it was in Srivijaya that he wrote his memoir of Buddhism during his own lifetime. Travellers to these islands mentioned that gold coinage was in use on the coasts, but not inland. A notable Srivijayan revered Buddhist scholar is
      <a href="/wiki/Dharmakirti" title="Dharmakirti">
       Dharmakirti
      </a>
      that taught Buddhist philosophy in Srivijaya and Nalanda, he was the teacher of Atisha.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_Commerce">
       Economy and Commerce
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Economy and Commerce">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_Expansion.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="657" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Buddhist_Expansion.svg/240px-Buddhist_Expansion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Buddhist_Expansion.svg/360px-Buddhist_Expansion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Buddhist_Expansion.svg/480px-Buddhist_Expansion.svg.png 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_Expansion.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Buddhist expansion from northern India to the rest of Asia, Srivijaya once served as a center of Buddhist learning and expansion. This expansion followed trade routes of Silk Road inland and maritime route.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the world of commerce, Srivijaya rapidly rose to be a far-flung empire controlling the two passages between India and China, namely the
      <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait">
       Sunda Strait
      </a>
      from Palembang and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malacca_strait" title="Malacca strait">
       Malacca strait
      </a>
      from Kedah. Arab accounts state that the empire of the maharaja was so vast that in two years the swiftest vessel could not travel round all its islands, which produced camphor, aloes, cloves, sandal-wood, nutmegs, cardamom and cubebs, ivory, gold and tin, making the maharaja as rich as any king in India.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poesponegoro_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Poesponegoro-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other than fostering the lucrative trade relations with India and China, Srivijaya also established commerce link with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">
       Arabia
      </a>
      . Highly possible, a messenger sent by Maharaja Sri Indravarman to deliver his letter for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">
       Caliph
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_AbdulAziz" title="Umar ibn AbdulAziz">
       Umar ibn AbdulAziz
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ummayad" title="Ummayad">
       Ummayad
      </a>
      in 718, was returned to Srivijaya with
      <i>
       Zanji
      </i>
      (black female slave from
      <a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">
       Zanj
      </a>
      ), the Caliph's present for maharaja. Later the Chinese chronicle mentioned about
      <i>
       Shih-li-t-'o-pa-mo
      </i>
      (Sri Indravarman), Maharaja of
      <i>
       Shih-li-fo-shih
      </i>
      in 724 had sent the emperor a
      <i>
       ts'engchi
      </i>
      (Chinese spelling of Arabic
      <i>
       Zanji
      </i>
      ) as a gift.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Azra_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Azra-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Decline">
       Decline
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Decline">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_kedah.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="305" data-file-width="300" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Battle_of_kedah.jpg/220px-Battle_of_kedah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Battle_of_kedah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Battle_of_kedah.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_kedah.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A Siamese painting depicting the Chola raid on Kedah.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Chola_invasion_of_Srivijaya" title="Chola invasion of Srivijaya">
       Chola invasion of Srivijaya
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The decline of Srivijaya was contributed by foreign piracy and raids that disrupted the trade and security in the region. Attracted to the wealth of Srivijaya, in 1025
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rajendra_Chola" title="Rajendra Chola">
       Rajendra Chola
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chola" title="Chola">
       Chola
      </a>
      king from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamilnadu" title="Tamilnadu">
       Tamilnadu
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">
       South India
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chola_invasion_of_Srivijaya" title="Chola invasion of Srivijaya">
       launched naval raids on ports of Srivijaya
      </a>
      and conquered
      <a href="/wiki/Kedah" title="Kedah">
       Kadaram
      </a>
      (modern Kedah) from Srivijaya. The Cholas are known to have benefitted from both
      <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">
       piracy
      </a>
      and foreign trade. Sometimes Chola seafaring led to outright plunder and conquest as far as Southeast Asia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An inscription of King Rajendra states that he captured Sangrama-vijayottungga-varman, the King of Kadaram, took a large heap of treasures including the Vidhyadara-torana, the jewelled 'war gate' of Srivijaya adorned with great splendour.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulke_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kulke-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Cholas continued a series of raids and conquests of parts of Sumatra and Malay Peninsula for the next 20 years. Several places in
      <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
       Malaysia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
       Indonesia
      </a>
      were invaded by
      <a href="/wiki/Rajendra_Chola_I" title="Rajendra Chola I">
       Rajendra Chola I
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Chola_dynasty" title="Chola dynasty">
       Chola dynasty
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The expedition of
      <a href="/wiki/Rajendra_Chola_I" title="Rajendra Chola I">
       Rajendra Chola I
      </a>
      had such a great impression to the Malay people of the medieval period that his name was mentioned in the corrupted form as Raja Chulan in the medieval Malay chronicle Sejarah Melaya.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buddhism.2C_Diplomacy_p.226_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Buddhism.2C_Diplomacy_p.226-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even today the Chola rule is remembered in
      <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
       Malaysia
      </a>
      as many
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malaysian" title="Malaysian">
       Malaysian
      </a>
      princes have names ending with Cholan or Chulan, one such was the Raja of
      <a href="/wiki/Perak" title="Perak">
       Perak
      </a>
      called
      <a href="/wiki/Raja_Chulan" title="Raja Chulan">
       Raja Chulan
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This event marked the demise of the Empire and a sharp turn for the control of the trade route. For the next century, Tamil trading companies from southern India dominated the Straits region, although the domination was weaker than the control of the Srivijayan Empire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singapore_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Singapore-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Rajendra overseas expedition against Srivijaya was a unique event in India's history and its otherwise peaceful relations with the states of Southeast Asia. The reasons of this naval expedition are still a moot point as the source are silent about its exact causes. Nilakanta Sastri suggests that the attack was probably caused by Srivijayan attempt to throw obstacles in the way of the Chola trade with the East, or more probably, a simple desire on the part of Rajendra to extend his
      <i>
       digvijaya
      </i>
      to the countries across the sea so well known to his subject at home, and therefore add luster to his crown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulke_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kulke-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although Srivijaya mandala still survive and the Chola invasion was ultimately unsuccessful, it gravely weakened the Srivijayan hegemony and enabled the formation of regional kingdoms, like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kediri_(historical_kingdom)" title="Kediri (historical kingdom)">
       Kediri
      </a>
      , based on intensive agriculture rather than coastal and long-distance trade. Srivijaya was humbled by this attack but not destroyed, the resilience of Srivijaya mandala still proven by the ascends of other royal members within Srivijaya mandala to step into power. With the time, the regional trading center shifted from the old Srivijayan capital of Palembang, to another trade center on the island of Sumatra,
      <a href="/wiki/Muaro_Jambi_Temple_Compounds" title="Muaro Jambi Temple Compounds">
       Jambi
      </a>
      , which was the center of
      <a href="/wiki/Melayu_Kingdom" title="Melayu Kingdom">
       Malayu
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singapore_69-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Singapore-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1100" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg/220px-Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg/330px-Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg/440px-Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chaiya_Wat_Kaew.jpg" title="Enlarge">

        <div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0">
         <div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left">

         </div>

      <tr>
       <td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
        <span class="metadata">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Thailand.svg">
          <img alt="Portal icon" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/16px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/24px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/32px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>
         </a>
        </span>

        </a>
