      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Thailand" title="Template:History of Thailand">
            <span title="View this template">
             v
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Thailand" title="Template talk:History of Thailand">
            <span title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Thailand&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Between 1079 and 1088, Chinese records show that Srivijaya sent ambassadors from Jambi and Palembang.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_165_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_165-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1079 in particular, an ambassador from Jambi and Palembang each visited China. Jambi sent two more ambassadors to China in 1082 and 1088.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_165_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_165-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This suggests that the centre of Srivijaya frequently shifted between the two major cities during that period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNOZ_165_70-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-MUNOZ_165-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Chola expedition as well as changing trade routes weakened Palembang, allowing Jambi to take the leadership of Srivijaya from the 11th century on.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to a Chinese source in the book of
      <i>
       Chu-fan-chi
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      written around 1225,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chou_Ju-kua" title="Chou Ju-kua">
       Chou Ju-kua
      </a>
      describe that in
      <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">
       Southeast Asia
      </a>
      archipelago there were two most powerful and richest kingdoms; Srivijaya and
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Java
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kediri_(historical_kingdom)" title="Kediri (historical kingdom)">
       Kediri
      </a>
      ). In Java he founds that the people adhere two kinds of religions:
      <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">
       Buddhism
      </a>
      and the religion of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmins" title="Brahmins">
       Brahmins
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">
       Hinduism
      </a>
      ), while the people of Srivijaya adhere to
      <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">
       Buddhism
      </a>
      . The people of Java are brave and short tempered, dare to put a fight. Their favourite pastimes was
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cockfighting" title="Cockfighting">
       cockfighting
      </a>
      and pig fighting. The currency was made from the mixture of
      <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">
       copper
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
       silver
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">
       tin
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The book of
      <i>
       Chu-fan-chi
      </i>
      mentioned that Java was ruled by a
      <a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">
       maharaja
      </a>
      , that rules several colonies: Pai-hua-yuan (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacitan" title="Pacitan">
       Pacitan
      </a>
      ), Ma-tung (Medang), Ta-pen (Tumapel, now
      <a href="/wiki/Malang" title="Malang">
       Malang
      </a>
      ), Hi-ning (
      <a href="/wiki/Dieng_Plateau" title="Dieng Plateau">
       Dieng
      </a>
      ), Jung-ya-lu (Hujung Galuh, now
      <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">
       Surabaya
      </a>
      ), Tung-ki (Jenggi,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Papua_(Indonesian_province)" title="West Papua (Indonesian province)">
       West Papua
      </a>
      ), Ta-kang (
      <a href="/wiki/Sumba" title="Sumba">
       Sumba
      </a>
      ), Huang-ma-chu (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papua_(Indonesian_province)" title="Papua (Indonesian province)">
       Southwest Papua
      </a>
      ), Ma-li (
      <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">
       Bali
      </a>
      ), Kulun (Gurun, identified as Gorong or
      <a href="/wiki/Sorong_Regency" title="Sorong Regency">
       Sorong
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Papua_(Indonesian_province)" title="West Papua (Indonesian province)">
       West Papua
      </a>
      or an island in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nusa_Tenggara" title="Nusa Tenggara">
       Nusa Tenggara
      </a>
      ), Tan-jung-wu-lo (
      <a href="/wiki/West_Kalimantan" title="West Kalimantan">
       Tanjungpura
      </a>
      in Borneo), Ti-wu (
      <a href="/wiki/Timor" title="Timor">
       Timor
      </a>
      ), Pingya-i (
      <a href="/wiki/Banggai_Island" title="Banggai Island">
       Banggai
      </a>
      in Sulawesi), and Wu-nu-ku (
      <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">
       Maluku
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      About Srivijaya, Chou-Ju-Kua
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      reported that Srivijaya had 15 colonies and was still the mightiest and wealthiest state in western part of archipelago. Srivijaya's colony are: Pong-fong (
      <a href="/wiki/Pahang" title="Pahang">
       Pahang
      </a>
      ), Tong-ya-nong (
      <a href="/wiki/Terengganu" title="Terengganu">
       Terengganu
      </a>
      ), Ling-ya-si-kia (
      <a href="/wiki/Langkasuka" title="Langkasuka">
       Langkasuka
      </a>
      ), Kilan-tan (
      <a href="/wiki/Kelantan" title="Kelantan">
       Kelantan
      </a>
      ), Fo-lo-an (
      <a href="/wiki/Dungun" title="Dungun">
       Dungun
      </a>
      , eastern part of Malay Peninsula, a town within state of Terengganu), Ji-lo-t'ing (
      <a href="/wiki/Cherating" title="Cherating">
       Cherating
      </a>
      ), Ts'ien-mai (Semawe, Malay Peninsula), Pa-t'a (
      <a href="/wiki/Paka,_Malaysia" title="Paka, Malaysia">
       Sungai Paka
      </a>
      , located in
      <a href="/wiki/Terengganu" title="Terengganu">
       Terengganu
      </a>
      of Malay Peninsula), Tan-ma-ling (
      <a href="/wiki/Tambralinga" title="Tambralinga">
       Tambralinga
      </a>
      , Ligor or
      <a href="/wiki/Nakhon_Si_Thammarat" title="Nakhon Si Thammarat">
       Nakhon Si Thammarat
      </a>
      , South
      <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
       Thailand
      </a>
      ), Kia-lo-hi (Grahi, (
      <a href="/wiki/Krabi_Province" title="Krabi Province">
       Krabi
      </a>
      ) northern part of Malay peninsula), Pa-lin-fong (
      <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">
       Palembang
      </a>
      ), Sin-t'o (
      <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Kingdom" title="Sunda Kingdom">
       Sunda
      </a>
      ), Lan-wu-li (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamuri" title="Lamuri">
       Lamuri
      </a>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">
       Aceh
      </a>
      ), Kien-pi (
      <a href="/wiki/Jambi" title="Jambi">
       Jambi
      </a>
      ) and Si-lan (
      <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">
       Cambodia
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muljana_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Muljana-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to this source in early 13th century Srivijaya still ruled Sumatra, Malay peninsula, and western Java (
      <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Kingdom" title="Sunda Kingdom">
       Sunda
      </a>
      ). About
      <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Kingdom" title="Sunda Kingdom">
       Sunda
      </a>
      , the book describe it further that the port of Sunda (probably refer to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bantam_(city)" title="Bantam (city)">
       Banten
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Kelapa" title="Sunda Kelapa">
       Sunda Kelapa
      </a>
      ) is really good and strategic,
      <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">
       pepper
      </a>
      from Sunda is among the best quality. People work on agriculture and their house are build on wooden piles (
      <i>
       rumah panggung
      </i>
      ). However the country was infested by robbers and thieves. In sum, this Chinese source from early 13th century suggested that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indonesian_archipelago" title="Indonesian archipelago">
       Indonesian archipelago
      </a>
      was ruled by two great kingdoms, with the western part under Srivijaya's rule, while eastern part was under Kediri domination.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1288,
      <a href="/wiki/Singhasari" title="Singhasari">
       Singhasari
      </a>
      , the successor of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kediri_(historical_kingdom)" title="Kediri (historical kingdom)">
       Kediri
      </a>
      in Java, conquered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malays_(ethnic_group)" title="Malays (ethnic group)">
       Melayu
      </a>
      state includes Palembang, Jambi as well as much of Srivijaya during the
      <a href="/wiki/Pamalayu_expedition" title="Pamalayu expedition">
       Pamalayu expedition
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In the year 1293,
      <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">
       Majapahit
      </a>
      ruled much of Sumatra as the successor of Singhasari. Prince
      <a href="/wiki/Adityawarman" title="Adityawarman">
       Adityawarman
      </a>
      was given responsibilities over
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumatera" title="Sumatera">
       Sumatera
      </a>
      in 1347 by
      <a href="/wiki/Tribhuwana_Wijayatunggadewi" title="Tribhuwana Wijayatunggadewi">
       Tribhuwana Wijayatunggadewi
      </a>
      , the third monarch of
      <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">
       Majapahit
      </a>
      . The rebellion in 1377 was squashed by
      <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">
       Majapahit
      </a>
      but it left the area of southern
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumatera" title="Sumatera">
       Sumatera
      </a>
      in chaos and desolation.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the following years, sedimentation on the Musi river estuary cut the kingdom's capital off from direct sea access. The strategic disadvantage crippled the trade in the Kingdom's capital. As the decline continued,
      <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
       Islam
      </a>
      made its way to the
      <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">
       Aceh
      </a>
      region of Sumatra, spreading through contacts with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
       Arab
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       Indian
      </a>
      traders. By the late 13th century, the kingdom of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pasai" title="Pasai">
       Pasai
      </a>
      in northern Sumatra converted to Islam. At the same time, Srivijayan lands in Malay Peninsula (now
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Thailand" title="Southern Thailand">
       Southern Thailand
      </a>
      ) was briefly a
      <a href="/wiki/Tributary_state" title="Tributary state">
       tributary state
      </a>
      of the Khmer empire and later the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sukhothai_kingdom" title="Sukhothai kingdom">
       Sukhothai kingdom
      </a>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      . The last inscription, on which a crown prince,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ananggavarman" title="Ananggavarman">
       Ananggavarman
      </a>
      , son of
      <a href="/wiki/Adityawarman" title="Adityawarman">
       Adityawarman
      </a>
      , is mentioned, dates from 1374.
     </p>
     <p>
      Several attempts to revive Srivijaya were made by the fleeing princes of Srivijaya.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      In 1324, a prince of Srivijaya origin, Sri Maharaja Sang Utama Parameswara Batara Sri Tribuwana (Sang Nila Utama) founded the ancient
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Singapura" title="Kingdom of Singapura">
       Kingdom of Singapura
      </a>
      (Temasek). He maintained control over Temasek for 48 years. Confirmed as ruler over Temasek by an envoy of the Chinese Emperor ca 1366. He was succeeded by his son Paduka Sri Pekerma Wira Diraja (13721386) and grandson, Paduka Seri Rana Wira Kerma (13861399). In 1401, his great grandson, Paduka Sri Maharaja
      <a href="/wiki/Parameswara_(sultan)" title="Parameswara (sultan)">
       Parameswara
      </a>
      was expelled from Temasek by a Majapahit invasion. He later headed north and founded
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Malacca" title="Sultanate of Malacca">
       Sultanate of Malacca
      </a>
      in 1402.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Malacca" title="Sultanate of Malacca">
       Sultanate of Malacca
      </a>
      succeeded Srivijaya Empire as a Malay political entity of the archipelago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
       Legacy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Srivijaya&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legacy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="480" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg/170px-Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg/255px-Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg/340px-Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borom_That_Chaiya.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Pagoda in Srivijaya style in Chaiya, Thailand
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although Srivijaya left few archaeological remains and was almost forgotten in the collective memory of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malay_(ethnic_group)" title="Malay (ethnic group)">
       Malay people
      </a>
      , the rediscovery of this ancient maritime empire by Coeds back in the 1920s stimulated the notion that it was possible in the past for a widespread political entity to thrive in Southeast Asia.
     </p>
     <p>
      The most important legacy of Srivijayan empire was probably their language. For centuries, Srivijaya through their expansion, economic power and military prowess was responsible for the widespread of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Malay" title="Old Malay">
       Old Malay
      </a>
      language throughout the Malay-Indonesian archipelago. It was the working language of traders, used in various ports and marketplaces in the region.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The language of Srivijayan was probably had paved the way for the prominence of present day
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
       Malay
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">
       Indonesian language
      </a>
      , to be the official language of Malaysia, Brunei and Singapore and as the unifying language of modern Indonesia.
     </p>
     <p>
      According to the
      <a href="/wiki/Malay_Annals" title="Malay Annals">
       Malay Annals
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Parameswara_(sultan)" title="Parameswara (sultan)">
       Parameswara
      </a>
      the founder of
      <a href="/wiki/Malacca_Sultanate" title="Malacca Sultanate">
       Malacca Sultanate
      </a>
      claimed to be the member of the Palembang Srivijaya lineage. This suggested that in the 15th century the prestige of Srivijaya still remained and was used as the source for political legitimacy in the region.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gadispalembang.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="332" data-file-width="500" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Gadispalembang.jpg/220px-Gadispalembang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Gadispalembang.jpg/330px-Gadispalembang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Gadispalembang.jpg/440px-Gadispalembang.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gadispalembang.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The gilded costume of South Sumatran
        <a href="/wiki/Gending_Sriwijaya" title="Gending Sriwijaya">
         Gending Sriwijaya
        </a>
        dance invoked the splendor of Srivijaya empire.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Modern
      <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
       Indonesian
      </a>
      nationalists have also invoked Srivijaya along with
      <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">
       Majapahit
      </a>
      , as a source of pride in Indonesia's past greatness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Srivijaya has become the focus of national pride and regional identity, especially for the people of
      <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">
       Palembang
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/South_Sumatra" title="South Sumatra">
       South Sumatra
      </a>
      province, and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malay_(ethnic_group)" title="Malay (ethnic group)">
       Malay people
      </a>
      as a whole. For the people of Palembang, Srivijaya has also become a source of artistic inspiration for
      <a href="/wiki/Gending_Sriwijaya" title="Gending Sriwijaya">
       Gending Sriwijaya
      </a>
      song and traditional dance.
     </p>
     <p>
      The same situation also happened in southern
      <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
       Thailand
      </a>
      , where
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sevichai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sevichai (page does not exist)">
       Sevichai
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">
       Thai
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="th" xml:lang="th">
       Srivijaya
      </span>
      ) dance was recreated in accordance with the art and culture of ancient Srivijaya. Today the Srivijayan legacy is also celebrated and identified with Malay minority of
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Thailand" title="Southern Thailand">
       Southern Thailand
      </a>
      . In Thailand, the Srivijayan art were associated with Javanese art and architecture, probably demonstrate the Sailendra influences over Java, Sumatra and the Peninsula. The examples of Srivijayan style temples are Phra Borom Mathat at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaiya" title="Chaiya">
       Chaiya
      </a>
      constructed in Javanese style made of brick and mortar (c. 9th 10th century), Wat Kaew Pagoda at Chaiya, also of Javanese form and Wat Long Pagoda. The original Wat Mahathat at
      <a href="/wiki/Nakhon_Si_Thammarat" title="Nakhon Si Thammarat">
       Nakhon Si Thammarat
      </a>
      (a Srivijayan city) was subsequently encased by a larger
      <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">
       Sri Lanka
      </a>
      styled building.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Indonesia, Srivijaya is a street name in many cities and has become synonymous with Palembang and South Sumatra.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Srivijaya_University" title="Srivijaya University">
       Srivijaya University
      </a>
      , established in 1960 in Palembang, was named after Srivijaya. Kodam Sriwijaya (a military commando area unit), PT Pupuk Sriwijaya (a fertilizer company), Sriwijaya Post (a Palembang-based newspaper),
      <a href="/wiki/Sriwijaya_Air" title="Sriwijaya Air">
       Sriwijaya Air
      </a>
      (an airline),
      <a href="/wiki/Gelora_Sriwijaya_Stadium" title="Gelora Sriwijaya Stadium">
       Gelora Sriwijaya Stadium
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sriwijaya_F.C." title="Sriwijaya F.C.">
       Sriwijaya F.C.
      </a>
      (Palembang football club) were also all named to honor this ancient maritime empire. On 11 November 2011 during the opening ceremony of
      <a href="/wiki/2011_Southeast_Asian_Games" title="2011 Southeast Asian Games">
       2011 Southeast Asian Games
      </a>
      in Gelora Sriwijaya Stadium, Palembang, a colossal dance performance titled "Srivijaya the Golden Peninsula" was performed featuring Palembang traditional dances and also an actual size replica of an ancient ship to describe the glory of this maritime empire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>

        </p>
